# The Wives of Prophet Muhammad ï·º: Comprehensive Biographies and Historical Significance

---

## Introduction

The role of the wives of Prophet Muhammad ï·º holds immense historical, social, and religious weight within Islamic tradition. Known as "Mothers of the Believers" (UmmahÄt al-MuÊ¾minÄ«n), these women were not merely consorts but influential figures who contributed profoundly to the formation and propagation of the early Muslim community. This comprehensive report presents an in-depth exploration of all the Prophetâ€™s wivesâ€”detailing their names, tribes, backgrounds, ages at marriage and death, and evaluating their religious, intellectual, and social impact on the nascent Islamic world. Drawing on classical SÄ«rah (prophetic biography) and other authoritative sources, alongside modern academic research and accessible online repositories, this report endeavors to provide a nuanced, thoroughly sourced account that recognizes both the individual legacy of each wife and the collective significance of their roles in Islamic history.

Islamic tradition underscores the importance of these women, as reflected in the Quran itself (Surah Al-Ahzab 33:6), which grants them a revered status among the Muslim ummah. Their stories, preserved in SÄ«rah, á¸¥adÄ«th, and later Islamic scholarship, offer insights not only into personal and familial dynamics but also into legislative, ethical, and social precedents that still resonate today.

---

## 1. Khadijah bint Khuwaylid (c. 554 â€“ 619 CE)

### Name and Background

Khadijah bint Khuwaylid, often honored as **Khadijah al-Kubra** ("the Great"), was from the prestigious Banu Asad clan of the Quraysh tribe in Mecca. Her father, Khuwaylid ibn Asad, was a successful merchant and a respected figure among the Quraysh. Her mother, Fatimah bint Za'idah, belonged to the Amir ibn Luayy clan. The familyâ€™s commercial ambition and social standing made Khadijah one of the wealthiest and most influential women in pre-Islamic Arabia.

### Age at Marriage and Death

Khadijahâ€™s precise age at marriage is debated but most traditional and contemporary sources place her at about **40 years old** at the time she married the Prophet, who was 25. She died in 619 CE, traditionally at age **64 or 65**, about a decade before the Prophetâ€™s own passing, in the year later known as the "Year of Sorrow" due to the compounded loss of both Khadijah and his uncle Abu Talib.

### Marriage and Family Life

Khadijah was widowed twice before her marriage to the Prophet, first to Abu Halah ibn Zurara at-Tamimi, then to 'Atiq ibn 'A'idh al-Makhzumi, from whom she bore children. With Prophet Muhammad ï·º, she had at least six childrenâ€”two sons (Qasim and Abdullah, neither of whom survived childhood) and four daughters (Zaynab, Ruqayyah, Umm Kulthum, and Fatimah). The Prophet had no other wife during Khadijah's lifetime, a testament to his affection and loyalty towards her.

### Key Contributions and Significance

Khadijahâ€™s contributions to early Islam are monumental. She was the **first believer** in the Prophetâ€™s message, supporting him emotionally and materially at critical junctures, particularly during the initial period of Revelation. Her unwavering faith and encouragement were so consequential that the Prophet himself repeatedly spoke of her virtues, stating she believed in him when others did not and supported him when others withheld support.

Her commercial acumen and generosity enabled Muhammad ï·º to propagate Islam while facing boycotts and persecution; her wealth supported the struggling Muslim community. Khadijah is one of the four women regarded as the greatest women of all times in Islamic tradition (the others being Maryam, mother of Jesus; Asiya, wife of Pharaoh; and Fatimah, Khadijahâ€™s daughter).

The loss of Khadijah marked a profound period of grief for the Prophet, indicative of her irreplaceable role in his lifeâ€”he did not remarry until after her death.

---

## 2. Sawdah bint Zam'ah (c. 566â€“580 â€“ 644/674 CE)

### Name and Background

Sawdah bint Zam'ah came from the Quraysh via the Banu Amir ibn Luâ€™ayy clan. Her father, Zamâ€™ah ibn Qays, was of Qurayshi descent, and her mother, Al-Shamus bint Qays, was of Khazraj origin from Medina, giving Sawdah close ties to two major tribes of Arabia.

### Age at Marriage and Death

Sawdahâ€™s age at the time of her marriage to the Prophet is estimated between **40 and 55 years**, most often cited as around **50**. She died either in **644** or as late as **674 CE**, making her between her early 60s and 100s at death, with dates varying in traditional records. She was buried in Jannat al-Baqi' in Medina.

### Marital and Migration History

Sawdah was first married to As-Sakran ibn Amr, an early Muslim. Together, they migrated to Abyssinia to escape Quraysh persecution. After her husbandâ€™s death, Sawdah returned, widowed and impoverished.

### Key Historical and Religious Contributions

After Khadijahâ€™s death, Sawdah was the Prophetâ€™s only wife for a period, marrying him in the tenth year of Prophethood. She is often praised for her sense of humor, loyalty, and piety. As a widow who had migrated for her faith, she represented the endurance and resilience of early Muslim women. Sawdah played an important role in caring for the Prophetâ€™s daughters and household in the difficult years following Khadijahâ€™s passing.

Notably, when Sawdah later feared the Prophet might divorce her due to her age, she offered her marital nights to Aisha, seeking to remain among the Prophetâ€™s wives and emphasizing the emotional solidarity and nobleness expected of the Mothers of the Believers.

---

## 3. Aisha bint Abi Bakr (c. 613/614 â€“ 678 CE)

### Name and Background

Aisha bint Abi Bakr was the daughter of **Abu Bakr as-Siddiq**, the Prophetâ€™s closest friend and first rightly-guided caliph after his death. Her lineage (Banu Taym clan of Quraysh) and close family ties placed her at the epicenter of the early Islamic community.

### Age at Marriage and Death

Sources indicate Aisha was **betrothed at around 6** and the marriage was consummated when she was **approximately 9 or 10 years old** (aged according to the lunar calendar). She lived until **678 CE**, dying at about **65** years old.

### Marriage and Role in the Prophetâ€™s Life

Aisha was the only wife of the Prophet to have not previously been married. She is often considered his most beloved spouse after Khadijah, based on multiple narrations; her proximity to him provided unique access to his domestic and legal teachings.

### Scholarly and Religious Contributions

Aishaâ€™s intelligence, eloquence, and memory made her one of the most significant narrators of hadithsâ€”being credited with transmitting over **2,000 hadiths** covering faith, law, and intimate matters of the Prophetâ€™s conduct. Her companionship extended far beyond the Prophetâ€™s life: she became an authority for major companions and the next generation of Muslims, teaching jurisprudence, poetry, medicine, and history. Her interpretations and legal judgments became precedents in Islamic jurisprudence.

Aisha played an active role in early Muslim political life, notably during the tumultuous events that followed the death of Uthman ibn Affan and during the Battle of the Camel (656 CE), a significant episode in Islamic civil history. Sunni tradition venerates her as a scholar and a model for female participation in societal affairs. Her legacy is more contested in Shiâ€™a tradition due to her political stance during the early schisms of the Muslim community, but her profound impact is acknowledged by all schools of thought.

---

## 4. Hafsah bint Umar (c. 605 â€“ 665 CE)

### Name and Background

Hafsah was the daughter of **Umar ibn al-Khattab**, the second caliph, one of the Prophetâ€™s closest companions, and a pivotal figure in early Islamic governance. Her mother was Zaynab bint Mazâ€™un, connecting her to notable Qurayshi lineages.

### Age at Marriage and Death

She was about **18 to 21 years old** when she married the Prophet in the third year after Hijrah (625 CE); her first husband, Khunays ibn Hudhafa, died after the Battle of Uhud. Hafsah lived until about **659/665 CE**, dying in her late 50s or around 60.

### Marriage, Character, and Legacy

The Prophetâ€™s marriage to Hafsah solidified ties with Umarâ€”her fatherâ€”and brought another literate, intellectually active woman into his household. Hafsah is known for her piety, courage, and literacy; she was one of the few women of her time who could read and write, and she played a crucial role in the *preservation of the Quran*. It was to Hafsah that the *written compiled manuscript of the Quran* (after Abu Bakr's collection) was entrusted until the time of the Caliph Uthman. This preservation was a critical contribution to the transmission of Islamâ€™s primary scripture and testifies to the trust placed in her.

Although less politically prominent than Aisha, Hafsah was active within the early Muslim community, known both for her frank character and her involvement in household and communal issues. She was buried in Jannat al-Baqi' in Medina.

---

## 5. Zaynab bint Khuzayma (c. 596 â€“ 625 CE)

### Name and Background

Zaynab bint Khuzayma was of the Banu Hilal, not the Quraysh, making her the first wife of the Prophet from outside the major Meccan clan. She was called "Umm al-Masakin", "Mother of the Poor," for her famed compassion and hospitality towards the needy.

### Age at Marriage and Death

She likely married the Prophet in her late **twenties**. Zaynab died within **8 months of marriage** in 625 CE (4 AH). She was buried in Medina, becoming the first of the Prophet's wives to be interred at al-Baqi'.

### Prior Marriages and Religious Significance

Zaynab had previously been married to several menâ€”perhaps At-Tufayl ibn al-Harith, Ubayda ibn al-Harith (who died at the Battle of Badr), and possibly Abdullah ibn Jahsh (who was martyred at Uhud), according to variant sources.

Her marriage to the Prophet is often viewed as both an act of compassion (to care for a deserving widow) and a social example, emphasizing the dignity of supporting those who had suffered for Islam. Despite her brief time as a wife of the Prophet, Zaynab left a strong impression due to her charity and self-abnegation.

---

## 6. Umm Salama (Hind bint Abi Umayya) (c. 580/596 â€“ 680/683 CE)

### Name and Background

Umm Salama, born Hind bint Abi Umayya, hailed from the noble Banu Makhzum clan of the Quraysh. Her father, Abu Umayya ibn al-Mughira, was known for his wealth and generosity.

### Age at Marriage and Death

Estimates for her birth range from **580 to 596 CE**. She married the Prophet in 4 AH (625/626 CE), after the death of her first husband, Abu Salama, during the Battle of Uhud. She lived until **680/683 CE**, dying at notably advanced ageâ€”some sources report close to **83**.

### Family, Migration, and Key Contributions

Before her marriage to the Prophet, Umm Salama was married to his close companion and foster brother, Abu Salama; their union produced four children. They were among the earliest converts and took part in the early migrations to Abyssinia and later to Medina.

Umm Salama is celebrated for her wisdom, eloquence, and depth of knowledge. She narrated a large number of hadiths and is respected for her insights into legal and social issues. Her role was particularly prominent after the Prophetâ€™s death, and she is regarded in some traditions as the most influential wife after Khadijah. Umm Salama was known to counsel and advise the Prophet himself in critical situations, including at Hudaybiyyah and the campaign of the Treaty of Peace, and she played a public role in advocating for women's rights as reflected in a famous Quranic verse (33:35), revealed after her inquiry about the status of women.

She lived the longest among the Prophetâ€™s wives, transmitting prophetic traditions and shaping the intellectual legacy of the early Muslim world.

---

## 7. Zaynab bint Jahsh (c. 590 â€“ 640/641 CE)

### Name and Background

Zaynab bint Jahsh was the Prophetâ€™s first cousin; her mother, Umayma bint Abd al-Muttalib, was a sister of the Prophetâ€™s father. Her father, Jahsh ibn Riyab, was from an immigrant tribe settled in Mecca. Zaynab was well known for her skill as a tanner and a craftswoman, a vocation she continued even after marriage.

### Age at Marriage and Death

Zaynab was born around **590** and married the Prophet in **627 CE** at the age of about **37**. She died in **640/641 CE** (aged approx. 51), buried at Jannat al-Baqi'.

### Unique Aspects of Her Marriage

Her marriage to the Prophet was the subject of direct Quranic revelation (Surah Al-Ahzab 33:37)â€”occurring after her divorce from Zayd ibn Haritha, the Prophetâ€™s adopted son. This union served a critical social-legal purpose: it abolished the pre-Islamic taboo against marrying the former wife of an adopted son, affirming that adopted sons were not the same as biological sons in legal terms.

### Key Contributions and Persona

Zaynab was exceptionally devout, charitable, and uprightâ€”she was reputed to spend much of her resources on the poor. Her piety and truthfulness received explicit praise both from the Prophet and from her co-wives (notably Aisha, with whom she was often compared). She narrates hadiths regarding domestic life, worship, and practices of charity, and her legacy exemplifies humility and service.

---

## 8. Juwayriya bint al-Harith (c. 608 â€“ 676 CE)

### Name and Background

Born Barrah bint al-Harith, Juwayriya was the daughter of the chief of **Banu Mustaliq**, a tribe defeated in battle by the Muslims in the fifth year of Hijrah.

### Age at Marriage and Death

She was likely **about 20 years old** when she became the Prophetâ€™s wife in **627 CE**, having been taken captive after the Battle of al-Muraysi'. She died in **676 CE** in Medina, aged approximately **68**.

### Marriage Circumstances and Significance

Originally a prisoner of war and recently widowed (her husband had died during the battle), Juwayriya approached the Prophet to negotiate her release. He offered to marry her instead, granting her freedom, which she accepted. This marriage had extensive social ramifications: as news of her new status spread, Muslims began to free dozens of captive families related to her, and her marriage is said to have led to the emancipation of an estimated one hundred families, fostering reconciliation between Muslims and Banu Mustaliq.

Juwayriya was known for her devout worshipâ€”Aisha noted her dedication to prayer and remembrance (dhikr)â€”and she later became an example of piety for other women of Medina.

---

## 9. Umm Habiba (Ramla bint Abi Sufyan) (c. 589/594 â€“ 665 CE)

### Name and Background

Ramla bint Abi Sufyan (Umm Habiba) was the daughter of **Abu Sufyan ibn Harb**, the prominent leader of the Umayyad clan and one of the Prophet's principal opponents prior to his eventual conversion. Her mother was Safiyyah bint Abi al-As.

### Age at Marriage and Death

Born around **589/594 CE**, she married the Prophet in **628 CE** when she was about **35** years old. She died in **665 CE**, estimated to be about **71** years old, and was buried at Jannat al-Baqi'.

### Life Events and Contributions

Umm Habibaâ€™s first marriage was to Ubaydullah ibn Jahsh, both early converts to Islam. The couple migrated to Abyssinia to escape Meccan persecution, but her husband apostatized to Christianity and died there, leaving her a widow with a young daughter.

Her marriage to the Prophet was arranged remotelyâ€”performed while she was still in Abyssinia and the Prophet in Medina, with the Christian king Negus presiding and providing a generous dower, demonstrating the Prophetâ€™s recognition of the importance of protecting displaced early Muslims and building connections to key figures in the nascent global community.

Umm Habiba is best remembered for her steadfastness in faith under hardship, her familial links to leading political figures of Islam's first century (her brother Muawiyah became the first Umayyad Caliph), and her roles in narrating hadith and in supporting the Prophetâ€™s household.

---

## 10. Safiyya bint Huyayy (c. 610 â€“ 670 CE)

### Name and Background

**Safiyya bint Huyayy** was of **Jewish** descent, daughter of Huyayy ibn Akhtab, the chief of Banu Nadir, and Barra bint Samaw'al of Banu Qurayza. She traced her ancestry to Aaron (Harun), brother of Moses, and belonged to the notable tribe of Levi.

### Age at Marriage and Death

Safiyya was likely **about 17** at the time of her capture and subsequent marriage to the Prophet after the *Battle of Khaybar* in **628 CE**. She died around **670 CE** in Medina, aged about **60**.

### Captivity, Conversion, and Marriage

She was previously married to Kenana ibn al-Rabi, who was killed at Khaybar. Following the defeat of the Jewish tribes, Safiyya was taken captive and offered the choice between freedom and Islam (with the option to return to her people), or marriage to the Prophet. She chose to accept Islam and marry the Prophet, who freed her as part of the marriage contract. Her inclusion among the Prophet's wives exemplifies Islam's expansion and integration of diverse ethnic and religious backgrounds into the early Muslim community.

### Significance

Safiyyaâ€™s position in the Prophetâ€™s household, as a convert and former elite of Jewish society, was not without tensionâ€”she reportedly faced taunts from co-wives but was consistently defended by the Prophet. She is remembered for her intelligence, beauty, and character, and transmitted hadiths on the Prophetâ€™s life. The Prophetâ€™s honorific treatment of her reinforced Islamâ€™s ethos of abolishing tribal bias and racial prejudice.

---

## 11. Maymunah bint al-Harith (c. 594 â€“ 671 CE)

### Name and Background

Maymunah (born Barra bint al-Harith) hailed from the Hawazin tribe. Her mother, Hind bint Awf, and her large extended family included many relatives who featured prominently in early Islamic history; she was half-sister to both Umm al-Fadl, mother of Abdullah ibn Abbas (the celebrated Qur'anic exegete), and Zaynab bint Khuzayma (another wife of the Prophet).

### Age at Marriage and Death

Maymunahâ€™s birth is placed around **594 CE**; she married the Prophet at **about age 36** after the death of her husband Abu Ruhm ibn Abd al-Uzza. The wedding took place at Sarif, outside Mecca, after the Prophetâ€™s â€˜Umrah al-Qadaâ€™ (the compensatory pilgrimage), in 629 CE. She lived until **671 CE**, reaching roughly **77 years of age**.

### Key Contributions

Maymunah was the last woman the Prophet married, referred to as the one who "had the greatest barakah" (blessing) for her marriage coincided with the Prophetâ€™s ability to re-enter Mecca after years of exile. She was devoted, pious, and played a significant part in preserving ties between different branches of the Prophetâ€™s family and companions. She narrated several hadiths and was acknowledged by Aisha for her piety and commitment to kinship.

---

## 12. Rayhana bint Zayd (died c. 631 CE)

### Name and Background

Rayhana bint Zayd was from the Jewish tribe of **Banu Nadir** and later married into the **Banu Qurayza**. She was captured during the siege of Banu Qurayza in 627 CE, following accusations of their tribeâ€™s collusion against the Muslims.

### Age at Marriage and Death

Rayhanaâ€™s age at the time of her marriage to the Prophet is not explicitly recorded, but she was likely relatively youngâ€”possibly in her twenties. She died around **631 CE**, shortly before the Prophetâ€™s death, and was buried in Medina.

### Contested Status

Rayhanaâ€™s precise status remains debated: some sources contend she was a wife, manumitted and married by the Prophet with a mahr (dowry); others describe her as a concubine, never formally wedded but remaining in his household until her death. Despite ambiguity, she is often included among the Mothers of the Believers in Sunni tradition due to her prominent position and the respect she received.

Rayhana initially resisted Islam out of loyalty to her late husband and religious heritage but eventually accepted Islam and chose to remain with the Prophet. She is remembered for her resilience and respected place in the Muslim community despite her harrowing early life experiences.

---

## 13. Maria al-Qibtiyya (died 637 CE)

### Name and Background

Maria bint ShamÊ¿Å«n, known as **Maria al-Qibtiyya**, was an Egyptian Christian slave sent as a gift to the Prophet by the ruler of Egypt, al-Muqawqis, after the Prophetâ€™s treaties and outreach to regional leaders. She hailed from the Coptic Christian community of upper Egypt, possibly of Greek and Coptic parentage.

### Status in the Prophetâ€™s Household

There is a scholarly debate as to whether Maria was a concubine or, as some sources claimâ€”particularly SÄ«rah worksâ€”a wife of the Prophet. Regardless, she was accorded full respect and enjoyed the privileges of a wife. Maria bore the Prophet a son, **Ibrahim**, his only child born in Medina; the child died in infancy, an event marked with grief by the Prophet and his companions.

### Conversion and Impact

Maria converted to Islam, and her sonâ€™s birth brought great joy to the Prophet, counteracting malicious claims from some opponents that he was â€œcut offâ€ from male progeny. Maria lived the rest of her life in Medina, honored by the Muslim community and ultimately buried at Jannat al-Baqi' cemetery. Her presence highlighted the Prophetâ€™s willingness to accept and uplift individuals from diverse backgrounds into the highest ranks of the early Muslim society.

| Safiyya bint Huyayy    | Banu Nadir (Jewish)    | 17              | 60          | Captive after Khaybar, noble lineage     | Widow                        | Bridge to diverse communities      |
| Maymunah bint al-Harith| Hawazin                | 36              | 77          | Large kinship network                    | Widow                        | Kinship, piety, tradition keeper   |
| Rayhana bint Zayd      | Banu Nadir (Jewish)    | 20s?            | ? (d. 631)  | War captive, ambiguity in status         | Widow                        | Conversion, steadfast faith        |
| Maria al-Qibtiyya      | Egypt (Coptic)         | ?               | 637         | Gift from Egypt, Christian convert       | Unmarried                    | Mother of Prophetâ€™s son Ibrahim    |

Each detail in this table is corroborated and expanded upon in the in-depth individual biographies and analyses.

---

## Collective Contributions and Legacy

Although the details of each wifeâ€™s life differ markedly, together they represent a spectrum of backgroundsâ€”Arab and non-Arab, Qurayshi and non-Qurayshi, wealthy merchants, noble prisoners of war, daughters of both friends and adversaries. They served as exemplars for believers through their **piety, wisdom, resilience, and social activism**.

The Prophetâ€™s marriages were shaped by multiple factorsâ€”*compassion towards the disadvantaged, consolidation of tribal alliances, elevation of social and ethical values, and legal reform.* His relationships with his wives reflected sensitivity to their needs, backgrounds, and aspirations, and promoted the social transformation of Arabian society. The women in his household set precedents in fields as diverse as **jurisprudence (Aisha, Hafsah, Umm Salama), charity (Khadijah, Zaynab bint Khuzayma, Zaynab bint Jahsh), diplomacy (Umm Habiba), social integration (Safiyya, Maria), and perseverance through hardship (Sawdah, Juwayriya, Rayhana)**.

Notably, the wivesâ€™ stories are also intertwined with broader Islamic concepts of womenâ€™s agency, scholarship, and participation in social and political lifeâ€”a theme highlighted by Aishaâ€™s activism and Umm Salamaâ€™s sage advice to the Prophet at Hudaybiyyah. These women, as "Mothers of the Believers," are revered in Islamic legal and cultural memory; their status is both symbolic and practical, setting ethical standards for relationships, social support, and leadership within the Muslim community.

---

## Conclusion

The wives of Prophet Muhammad ï·º are more than names inscribed in the annals of Islamic history. Each, in her unique way, shaped the social, legal, and spiritual contours of the early Muslim community. Their biographies, preserved in canonical SÄ«rah, hadith, and rich interpretive traditions, remain critical to understanding Islamic ideals of charity, fidelity, wisdom, education, endurance, and compassion. They remain models for all generations of Muslimsâ€”reminding the ummah of the diversity, dignity, and excellence that characterized the household of the Messenger of God. Their lives demand ongoing study, reflection, and emulation by individuals and communities everywhere.

---

These sources provided diverse perspectives, rich context, and additional verification for each cited fact.

---

**In summary, the collective and individual stories of the Prophetâ€™s wives offer enduring lessons in faith, service, and universal human dignityâ€”qualities that continue to inspire billions of Muslims worldwide.**

## ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§ğŸš§

# Is Cooking with Extra Virgin Olive Oil Harmful or Safe? A Comprehensive Analysis Based on Medical Research and Scientific Literature

---

## Introduction

The use of extra virgin olive oil (EVOO) in culinary traditions, especially as a staple of the Mediterranean diet, has been both widely celebrated and controversially debated in recent years. While its raw consumption is universally touted for its health benefits, considerable uncertainty persists regarding its safety and efficacy when subjected to heat during cooking. One of the most pervasive concerns is related to its smoke pointâ€” the temperature at which oil begins to smoke and potentially yield harmful byproducts. Complicating the public discourse, many draw analogies between the dangers of overheated oil and those of consuming smoked foods, like brisket, influencing perceptions about the risks or myths attached to using EVOO for sautÃ©ing, frying, and roasting.

This report delivers an exhaustive exploration anchored in peer-reviewed medical studies, nutrition science texts, and recommendations from leading health authorities. It interrogates the health risks and benefits of cooking with EVOO, clarifies the scientific basis (or lack thereof) for concerns about its smoke point, compares the chemistry of oil smoke with that of smoked foods, and surveys both sides of the evolving debate. Furthermore, it outlines proven safe cooking methods and evaluates public perceptions and misinformation, ensuring a nuanced, evidence-based perspective.

---

## Health Risks of Cooking with Extra Virgin Olive Oil

### Oxidative Stability and Formation of Harmful Compounds Under Heat

A primary concern frequently cited is the potential for EVOO, being predominantly composed of unsaturated fatty acids, to oxidize when heated, thus generating harmful compounds such as polar compounds and aldehydes. These substances, if produced in large amounts, have been associated in some studies with an increased risk of inflammation, cellular damage, and long-term diseases such as cancer or neurodegenerative disorders.

However, rigorous evaluations in laboratory and real-world cooking conditions have consistently found that EVOO demonstrates *exceptional oxidative stability* when compared to many commonly used vegetable and seed oils, especially under temperatures typical for home cooking (below 200â€“210Â°C/392â€“410Â°F). For example, a landmark study published in *Acta Scientific Nutritional Health* subjected EVOO, canola, grapeseed, and other oils to high heat over prolonged periods (such as 6 hours at 180Â°C/356Â°F). Results revealed that EVOO generated the *lowest levels of polar compounds and oxidative byproducts*, in contrast to the significantly higher and more harmful byproduct levels detected in seed oils like canola and sunflower.

In summary, while all oils degrade when repeatedly or excessively overheated, EVOOâ€™s fatty acid profileâ€”rich in monounsaturated fatsâ€”and its abundant natural antioxidants (polyphenols) confer a resistance to harmful oxidation rivaling, if not surpassing, that of refined high-smoke-point oils. Routine culinary heating does not, in and of itself, result in the production of dangerous toxins at levels proven to harm human health.

### The Impact of Exceeding the Smoke Point

The greatest risk arises when any oil, including EVOO, is *heated beyond its smoke point* for extended periods. At this stage, not only do health-promoting antioxidants degrade, but marker compounds such as *acrolein*, a respiratory irritant and possible carcinogen, and free radicals, may also be produced. Thus, while brief, minor episodes of smoke are unlikely to be significant in home cooking, deliberate or repeated overheating is discouraged by health authorities.

### Loss of Beneficial Compounds

Although EVOO preserves many of its key phenolic compounds and monounsaturated fats during moderate heating, some sensitive antioxidants and flavor volatiles do diminish with increased heat exposure. For most cooking applications, the loss is minimal; however, extremely high temperatures or prolonged frying can reduce the antioxidant content further. Even so, measurable health promoting polyphenols, like *oleocanthal*, survive typical home cooking processes according to current research.

### Cardiovascular and Metabolic Risks Attributed to Heated Oils

The longstanding claim that fried foods are inherently associated with increased cardiovascular risk has also been tested specifically for EVOO. Comprehensive reviews and randomized clinical trials indicate no increased risk of heart disease when olive oil is used for frying, especially in contrast to animal fats or polyunsaturated-rich seed oils. Notably, the large-scale *PREDIMED* trial found that substituting animal fats with extra virgin olive oil, even in fried foods, correlated with a decreased incidence of cardiovascular events.

### Public Health Organization Assessments

Organizations such as the American Heart Association and Harvard Health Publishing view olive oil (specifically extra virgin) as among the safest choices for heart health, including for most cooking methods, with the crucial caveats of avoiding excessive or repeated overheating and ensuring the oil is not rancid.

---

## Health Benefits of Extra Virgin Olive Oil Consumption

### Cardiovascular Protection and Longevity

The role of EVOO in promoting cardiovascular health is among the most firmly established benefits in medical literature. Multiple meta-analyses and cohort studies robustly correlate higher olive oil consumption (often, just over Â½ tablespoon per day) with marked reductions in cardiovascular events and all-cause mortality. For instance, a 2022 Harvard study, which pooled data from more than 92,000 individuals over 28 years, showed that the highest olive oil consumers had a 19% lower risk of death from all causes, and up to 34% lower mortality when olive oil replaced saturated fats like butter, reflecting olive oilâ€™s unique nutritional profile.

### Anti-Inflammatory and Antioxidant Properties

The health benefits stem primarily from EVOOâ€™s high content of monounsaturated fats (notably oleic acid) and unique antioxidant polyphenols, such as oleuropein, hydroxytyrosol, and oleocanthal. These polyphenols impart EVOO with strong anti-inflammatory and antioxidant capacity, reducing chronic inflammation, oxidative stress, and, by extension, the risk of chronic diseases including some cancers, neurodegeneration, and metabolic syndrome.

### Blood Lipid Improvement

While the effects of olive oil consumption on serum lipid profiles (cholesterol and triglyceride levels) are statistically modest according to systematic meta-analyses of randomized controlled trials, the direction is consistently favorable: minimal increases in HDL ("good") cholesterol and slight decreases in LDL ("bad") cholesterol and triglycerides have been observed. The broader benefit appears to be a reduction in oxidized LDL and improved endothelial function, suggestive of enhanced vascular health.

### Additional Disease Prevention

Systematic reviews point to protective associations between olive oil consumption and the risk of type 2 diabetes, certain cancers, and neurodegenerative conditions such as Alzheimerâ€™s disease. These benefits are most pronounced when EVOO is consumed regularly within a Mediterranean dietary pattern.

---

## Smoke Point Concerns: Science, Myths, and Reality

### Understanding Smoke Point

The *smoke point* of an oil is the temperature at which it begins to visibly smoke, indicating decomposition and the liberation of volatile compounds, including acrolein and free fatty acids. Traditional guidance has advised against heating EVOO on the basis that its purportedly low smoke point would increase the risk of generating unhealthy substances.

However, modern research has challenged the primacy and relevance of smoke point as the sole metric for judging an oilâ€™s safety under heat. In fact, the smoke point of extra virgin olive oil is *not* always as low as once thoughtâ€”it generally ranges from 190Â°C (374Â°F) to 215Â°C (419Â°F), depending on freshness, quality, and free fatty acid content.

#### Table 1: Typical Smoke Points of Common Cooking Oils

| Oil Type                         | Typical Smoke Point (Â°C/Â°F)       | Key Notes                         |
|----------------------------------|-----------------------------------|-----------------------------------|
| Extra Virgin Olive Oil (EVOO)    | 190â€“215Â°C / 374â€“419Â°F             | Depends on quality/age            |
| Refined Olive Oil                | 199â€“243Â°C / 390â€“470Â°F              | Higher due to lower acidity       |
| Avocado Oil (Refined)            | 271Â°C / 520Â°F                     | Highest among common oils         |
| Sunflower Oil (Refined)          | 252â€“254Â°C / 486â€“489Â°F              | High, but low oxidative stability |
| Canola Oil                       | 204Â°C / 400Â°F                     | High, lower stability than EVOO   |
| Butter                           | 150Â°C / 302Â°F                     | Very low, not for high-heat use   |

**Summary Table Context**: Although EVOOâ€™s smoke point is lower than highly refined oils, it is sufficiently high for most home cooking techniques, such as sautÃ©ing, baking, and even shallow frying. Its smoke point is rarely exceeded during typical culinary preparation, as the actual cooking temperature of oil in real-life kitchen usage seldom approaches these upper thresholds.

### The Weak Correlation Between Smoke Point and Oil Safety

Several contemporary studies, especially the frequently cited 2018 investigation conducted by Modern Olives Laboratory Services and published in *Acta Scientific Nutritional Health*, have demonstrated that *smoke point alone does not reliably predict the formation of harmful compounds or the overall stability of an oil under heat*. Instead, an oilâ€™s oxidative stabilityâ€”affected by its fatty acid profile and antioxidant contentâ€”is far more consequential.

EVOO, due to its high level of monounsaturated fats and robust polyphenol concentration, consistently outperforms oils with higher smoke points that lack antioxidants and contain more polyunsaturated fats, which oxidize faster and produce more toxic byproducts when exposed to heat.

### When Does the Smoke Point Matter?

Problems arise if oil is *continuously overheated*â€”for example, heating until it regularly and vigorously emits smoke, especially if sustained for an extended period. At that point, the breakdown of triglycerides yields not only free radicals and acrolein but also contributes to flavor degradation. Nevertheless, such abuse is atypical during regular home cooking. Experts generally advise only discarding oil that has been allowed to smoke excessively or repeatedly, a recommendation echoed by both culinary professionals and health authorities.

---

## Chemical Stability and Oxidation Products Compared to Other Cooking Oils

### Why EVOO Demonstrates High Heat Resilience

The central reason for EVOO's stability is its unique composition:
- **Monounsaturated fatty acids (MUFAs)**: These account for about 73% of EVOOâ€™s fat content (mainly oleic acid), which are less susceptible to heat-induced oxidation than polyunsaturated fats found in seed oils.
- **Polyphenols and Antioxidants**: Natural antioxidants act as internal stabilizers, neutralizing free radicals and preventing rapid formation of polar compounds during heating.
- **Low Free Fatty Acidity**: High-quality, fresh EVOO has minimal free fatty acids, which correlates with a higher smoke point and better oxidative stability.

These factors mean that, even after moderate heating, a significant proportion of healthful compoundsâ€”including polyphenols, squalene, and oleocanthalâ€”remain present and active in the oil and dishes prepared with it.

### Comparative Degradation Profiles

Under controlled heating, studies uniformly show that EVOO generates less than half the amount of harmful polar compounds as canola, sunflower, and other commonly used seed oils. In fact, even after six hours at 180Â°C (356Â°F), EVOOâ€™s byproduct levels were substantially the lowest, with coconut oil being a distant second in most trials.

In contrast, *polyunsaturated-rich* oilsâ€”despite often having higher smoke pointsâ€”rapidly produce aldehydes and other toxic degradation products under similar conditions, making them less suitable for high-heat cooking from a health perspective.

---

## Smoke Point Concerns Versus the Chemistry of Smoked Foods

### The Chemistry of Oil Smoke Versus Food Smoke

There is frequent confusion between the risks of cooking oils â€œsmokingâ€ and the health hazards associated with consuming smoked foods such as brisket or smoked fish. However, these processes are chemically and physiologically distinct.

#### Oil Smoke: What Actually Happens?

When oilsâ€”including EVOOâ€”are overheated past their smoke points, they decompose into a mixture of compounds: acrolein (irritating to the eyes/respiratory tract), simple carbon fragments, and, most relevantly, *polar oxidative byproducts*. In general, these byproducts are only present in potentially harmful concentrations if the oil is repeatedly abused at very high temperatures or re-used many times, as is sometimes seen in commercial or deep-frying practices.

#### Smoked Foods: A Different Mechanism

In contrast, the hazards of smoked foodsâ€”such as brisketâ€”arise from the deposition of *polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs)* and *heterocyclic amines (HCAs)*. These carcinogenic substances are created when fat and juices from meat drip onto hot surfaces or flames, prompting chemical reactions in the presence of intense, direct high heat and smoke exposure. The smoke carries these chemicals, which then adhere to the surface of the meat.

PAHs and HCAs are both mutagenic (capable of damaging DNA) and have been positively associated with increased risks for multiple cancers in laboratory and epidemiological studies. The World Health Organization classifies processed and smoked meats as Group 1 carcinogens, explicitly due to these contaminants.

#### Risk Magnitude and Context

Though both processes involve high heat, the chemicals generated and health consequences are distinct. PAHs in smoked foods exist in considerably higher concentrations and exhibit a much stronger association with negative health outcomes than the relatively small amounts of irritants and polar compounds generated by brief or moderate overheating of cooking oils.

---

## Safe Cooking Methods with Extra Virgin Olive Oil

### Best Practices for Health and Flavor

- **Stay Below the Smoke Point:** For everyday sautÃ©ing, roasting, or baking, keep cooking temperatures below 200Â°C/392Â°F (for most EVOOs, this is easily manageable). Most stovetop and oven recipes do not exceed this temperature; even on high flame, pan temperatures rarely approach oilâ€™s smoke point when food is present.
- **Do Not Preheat Empty Pans of Oil Excessively:** Adding EVOO to a hot pan, rather than preheating oil for extended periods, reduces the risk of overheating and smoke.
- **Use Fresh, High-Quality EVOO:** Fresh oil (well within â€œbest byâ€ date, stored in a cool, dark place) not only delivers better flavor but has greater heat stability and a higher smoke point due to lower free fatty acid content.
- **Avoid Re-using Oil:** Cooking with fresh oil in each batch substantially reduces the risk of accumulating harmful byproducts. Unlike commercial deep fryers, home practices rarely achieve problematic levels with single or double use.
- **Store EVOO Properly:** Exposure to air, light, and heat accelerate rancidity and breakdown of antioxidantsâ€”so store oil in dark bottles, sealed tight, and away from heat sources.
- **Use for Low and Moderate-Heat Cooking:** EVOO is ideal for sautÃ©ing, light frying, baking, and finishing dishes. For extremely high-heat applications (such as deep-frying above 210Â°C/410Â°F), choose a light tasting or refined olive oil or avocado oil, though such use is rare in home kitchens.

### Techniques to Maximize Health Benefits

- **Add EVOO at the End:** In dishes where heat is sustained for a long period, consider adding a drizzle of fresh EVOO at the end of cooking to maximize the intake of thermolabile polyphenols and aromatic compounds.
- **Combine with Vegetables:** Frying or roasting vegetables in EVOO not only preserves antioxidants but has been shown to increase the bioavailability of certain nutrients and polyphenols that are otherwise lost during boiling or steaming.

---

## Myth Versus Fact: Olive Oil Cooking Safety Analysis

### The Myth of Unsuitability for Cooking

**Myth:** "Olive oil, especially extra virgin, should never be heated, as doing so destroys its health benefits or creates toxins."

**Fact:** Comprehensive research conclusively demonstrates that EVOO remains stable during normal culinary heating and retains significant health benefits. Excessive or repeated overheating, which is unlikely with typical kitchen techniques, does initiate greater breakdown, but EVOO still outperforms most seed oils in the amount of harmful byproducts produced under the same conditions.

### The Role of Social Media and Influencer Narratives

Partly due to historical issues with the import and quality of olive oilsâ€”particularly in North Americaâ€”wellness influencers and social media enthusiasts have promulgated the idea that EVOO is unsafe for cooking, referencing out-of-context chemistry or anecdotal misperceptions. Detailed myth-busting guides from university food science programs, medical organizations, and nutrition experts counter these claims, reiterating the safety and benefits of using EVOO within its smoke point for cooking.

### Evidence from Medical and Professional Sources

Every major nutrition authority, including the American Heart Association, Harvard Health, and the British Heart Foundation, currently lists EVOO as one of the healthiest oils for both raw consumption and moderate-temperature cooking, highlighting that concerns about smoke point have been overstated in popular media.

### Misperceptions About Loss of Nutrients and Harmful Compounds

Some nutrient loss (mainly sensitive polyphenols) does occur with heating, but the main antioxidant and monounsaturated fat content enduresâ€”especially compared to other oils which lose their beneficial properties (and generate more toxic byproducts) even more rapidly.

### Comparative Safety with Other Common Oils

Evidence now squarely establishes that refined seed oils, despite sometimes offering higher smoke points, are less stable, lose their beneficial nutrients more rapidly, and produce higher levels of harmful oxidized byproducts when subjected to the same heating protocols.

---

## Summary Table: Key Findings from Recent Medical and Scientific Sources

| Aspect                          | Evidence/Analysis                                                               | Scientific Consensus                                     | Noteworthy Sources                                  |
|----------------------------------|--------------------------------------------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------|-----------------------------------------------------|
| **Health Risks**                | Brief or moderate heating does NOT generate harmful levels of toxins. Sustained overheating and repeated use do, but at a lower rate than seed oils. | Safe for sautÃ©ing, baking, light frying; avoid smoking oil. | Acta Scientific 2018, Harvard Health, AHA, UC Davis |
| **Health Benefits**             | EVOO reduces CVD risk, improves inflammation, and contains robust antioxidants, many preserved after cooking. | Regular use in cooking and diets like Mediterranean improves longevity, disease prevention. | Harvard, JACC, Nutrients 2025, PREDIMED Trial      |
| **Smoke Point**                 | Ranges 190â€“215Â°C (374â€“419Â°F) depending on quality; sufficient for most home cooking. | Smoke point not sole predictor of safety; oxidative stability is more meaningful. | Acta Scientific 2018, Modern Olives Lab, U.S. News  |
| **Chemical Stability**          | High MUFA and polyphenol content reduce formation of toxic polar compounds compared to seed oils. | EVOO outperforms canola, grapeseed, sunflower oils under high-heat. | Acta Scientific, Olive Wellness Inst., Diet vs Disease |
| **Comparison with Smoked Foods**| Oil smoke mainly acrolein/free radicals; smoked meats accrue PAHs/HCAs, carcinogenic at much higher concentrations. | Smoking of meat yields much higher risk due to PAHs/HCAs than moderate oil smoke. | NCI, WHO, EFSA, BJN, New Scientist                 |
| **Safe Cooking Methods**        | Use fresh, quality EVOO, avoid reuse or deep overheating, store properly for stability. | Safe for all typical kitchen techniques except high-temp deep frying. | Harvard, AHA, UC Davis, New Scientist               |
| **Myth vs Fact**                | Social media exaggerates risks; most professional/medical sources confirm safety and benefits. | Major global health authorities approve EVOO for cooking. | Harvard, Olive Oil Diet Book, UC Davis, NAOOA       |

---

## Conclusion

Based on a synthesis of contemporary peer-reviewed research, authoritative nutrition science texts, and clear guidance from global health organizations, **cooking with extra virgin olive oil is not only safe but offers distinct health advantages when properly used**. The core concern about its smoke point is scientifically overblown: most quality EVOO offers a smoke point well above everyday cooking temperatures, and its unrivalled oxidative stability means it yields significantly fewer harmful byproducts than the seed oils that typically supplant it based on outdated smoke point fears.

Consuming truly â€œsmokedâ€ foods, on the other hand, exposes individuals to a vastly different and more dangerous array of carcinogenic substancesâ€”chief among them PAHs and HCAsâ€”which cannot be equated with minor, brief oil smoking in normal kitchen use. Thus, the analogy between oil smoke and smoked brisket is scientifically inaccurate.

The most prudent and health-promoting practices are to use fresh, high-quality extra virgin olive oil for sautÃ©ing, roasting, baking, and finishing dishes, being mindful not to allow the oil to smoke excessively or to reuse it many times. By adhering to these principles, home cooks can harness both the incomparable flavor and well-established health benefits of EVOO, remaining confident in its safety for typical culinary applications.

Ultimately, the notion that extra virgin olive oil should not be cooked with is a persistent *myth*, unsupported by rigorous scientific evidence. On the contrary, EVOO is among the safest and healthiest oils for most forms of home cookingâ€” a finding consistently echoed by experts, large-scale clinical studies, and global dietary recommendations.

---

**Key Takeaways:**  
- **No scientific evidence suggests that moderate cooking with extra virgin olive oil poses a health risk.**
- **Smoke point is not the decisive criterion for oil safety; oxidative stability and antioxidant retention are more relevant.**
- **EVOO outperforms most oils in retaining health benefits and minimizing harmful byproduct formation during cooking.**
- **Smoked foods pose uniquely higher health risks due to PAH/HCAs, unrelated to the minor risks associated with overheated oils.**
- **Proper selection, storage, and mindful use of EVOO render it safe and health-promoting for culinary applications.**

---

## Further Reading

For readers seeking detailed technical data, comprehensive recipes, or advanced clinical trial interpretations, recommended resources include *The Olive Oil Diet* by Dr. Simon Poole and Judy Ridgway, the Olive Wellness Institute white papers, and peer-reviewed journals such as *Nutrition Reviews*, *British Journal of Nutrition*, and *Frontiers in Nutrition*.

# Compilation of Hadiths Related to Anger and Temper from Sunnah.com

---

## Introduction

Anger is a universally experienced emotion and its management is a persistent ethical concern across cultures and religious traditions. In Islamic teaching, the Qurâ€™an and Hadith literature provide comprehensive guidance on how to understand and mitigate the destructive effects of uncontrolled anger. The Prophet Muhammad ï·º is recorded in many hadiths as cautioning against anger, describing its spiritual dangers, and recommending practical and spiritual methods for control. This report presents an exhaustive collection of hadiths specifically related to anger and temper, extracted from key canonical sources as found on Sunnah.com. It includes both translated and Arabic-only narrations, providing new translations where necessary and including direct links to each hadith's source.

Each hadith is presented under a clear heading, with its full English translation and a direct URL to the original entry on Sunnah.com. For Arabic-only hadiths, the translation is provided in this report. The organization and elaboration ensure completeness, clarity, and ease of navigation for researchers, educators, and anyone seeking comprehensive Islamic guidance on the subject of anger.

---

## **1. True Strength Lies in Controlling Anger**

### **Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1317 & Sahih al-Bukhari 6114**

**Narration:**  
Abu Hurayra reported that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said:  
*"The person who is strong is not strong because he can knock people down. The person who is strong is the one who controls himself when he is angry."*

- [Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1317 (Book 57, Hadith 1)](https://sunnah.com/adab/57/1)
- [Sahih al-Bukhari 6114 (Book 78, Hadith 141)](https://sunnah.com/bukhari/78/141)
- [Bulugh al-Maram - The Comprehensive Book (Book 16, Hadith 45)](https://sunnah.com/urn/2054430)

**Elaboration:**  
This pivotal hadith recurs throughout the canonical collections, emphasizing the core Islamic ethic that strength is not in overcoming others through physical might but lies in mastering one's own impulses at the moment of anger. The Prophet ï·º redefined the cultural notion of severity and masculinity, making self-restraint in the face of anger a mark of true courage and virtue. It is a concise teaching that underscores both emotional intelligence and spiritual maturity, qualities that are repeatedly praised throughout the Sunnah and are integral to maintaining family, social, and communal harmony.

---

## **2. The Greatest Reward for Swallowing Anger**

### **Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1318**

**Narration:**  
Ibn 'Umar said:  
*"There is nothing that is swallowed greater with Allah in reward than a slave of Allah who swallows and contains his rancour out of desire for the pleasure of Allah."*

- [Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1318 (Book 57, Hadith 2)](https://sunnah.com/adab/57/2)

**Elaboration:**  
Ibn 'Umar's report, while not a direct statement from the Prophet ï·º, is given prominent placement in collections due to its spiritual insight. The use of "swallowing" for anger metaphorically shows that its suppression, especially for Allahâ€™s sake, carries immense divine reward. This theme recurs in many moral traditions, but its placement here demonstrates the Islamic approach: that spiritual advancement and reward are tied to the internal struggle (jihad al-nafs) against destructive emotions. By containing anger, one not only brings peace to interpersonal relations but also earns high standing with Allah.

---

## **3. Words to Say When Angry: Seeking Refuge from Shaytan**

### **Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1319 / Sahih al-Bukhari 3282 / Jami` at-Tirmidhi 3452 / Sunan Abi Dawud 4780**

**Narration:**  
Sulayman ibn Surad said:  
*"Two men abused one another in the presence of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and one of them began to get angry and his face got red. The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, looked at him and said, 'I know some words that, if he says them, will remove this from him. They are: "I seek refuge with Allah from the Accursed Shaytan."' The man went to that man and said, 'Do you know what he said? He said, "I seek refuge with Allah from the Accursed Shaytan."' The man retorted. 'Do you think me mad?'"*

- [Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1319 (Book 57, Hadith 3)](https://sunnah.com/adab:1319)
- [Sahih al-Bukhari 3282 (Book 59, Hadith 91)](https://sunnah.com/bukhari:3282)
- [Jami` at-Tirmidhi 3452 (Book 48, Hadith 83)](https://sunnah.com/tirmidhi/48/83)
- [Sunan Abi Dawud 4780 (Book 43, Hadith 8)](https://sunnah.com/abudawud:4780)

**Elaboration:**  
The recurrence of this episode in various authentic hadith collections lends it unparalleled weight in Islamic pedagogy. The Prophet ï·º identifies the spiritual root of destructive angerâ€”namely, the influence of Shaytan (Satan). He prescribes a simple spiritual remedy: seeking refuge in Allah from Shaytan. The additional details in some versions, such as the visible physical symptoms of anger and the manâ€™s stubbornness, illustrate both the universality of anger and the practical challenge of adopting prophetic advice in heated moments. The hadith teaches Muslims to be conscious of spiritual influences behind strong emotions and provides actionable, faith-based strategies for regaining composure.

---

## **4. A Variant of the Previous Hadith**

### **Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1319b**

**Original Arabic:**  
ÙƒÙÙ†Ù’ØªÙ Ø¬ÙØ§Ù„ÙØ³Ù‹Ø§ Ù…ÙØ¹Ù Ø§Ù„Ù†Ù‘ÙØ¨ÙÙŠÙ‘Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù… ÙˆÙØ±ÙØ¬ÙÙ„Ø§ÙÙ†Ù ÙŠÙØ³Ù’ØªÙØ¨Ù‘ÙØ§Ù†ÙØŒ ÙÙØ£ÙØ­ÙØ¯ÙÙ‡ÙÙ…ÙØ§ Ø§Ø­Ù’Ù…ÙØ±Ù‘Ù ÙˆÙØ¬Ù’Ù‡ÙÙ‡ÙØŒ ÙˆÙØ§Ù†Ù’ØªÙÙÙØ®ÙØªÙ’ Ø£ÙÙˆÙ’Ø¯ÙØ§Ø¬ÙÙ‡ÙØŒ ÙÙÙ‚ÙØ§Ù„Ù Ø§Ù„Ù†Ù‘ÙØ¨ÙÙŠÙ‘Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù…â€:â€ Ø¥ÙÙ†Ù‘ÙÙŠ Ù„ÙØ£ÙØ¹Ù’Ù„ÙÙ…Ù ÙƒÙÙ„ÙÙ…ÙØ©Ù‹ Ù„ÙÙˆÙ’ Ù‚ÙØ§Ù„ÙÙ‡ÙØ§ Ù„ÙØ°ÙÙ‡ÙØ¨Ù Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’Ù‡Ù Ù…ÙØ§ ÙŠÙØ¬ÙØ¯ÙØŒ ÙÙÙ‚ÙØ§Ù„ÙÙˆØ§ Ù„ÙÙ‡Ùâ€:â€ Ø¥ÙÙ†Ù‘Ù Ø§Ù„Ù†Ù‘ÙØ¨ÙÙŠÙ‘Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù… Ù‚ÙØ§Ù„Ùâ€:â€ ØªÙØ¹ÙÙˆÙ‘ÙØ°Ù’ Ø¨ÙØ§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù Ù…ÙÙ†Ù Ø§Ù„Ø´Ù‘ÙÙŠÙ’Ø·ÙØ§Ù†Ù Ø§Ù„Ø±Ù‘ÙØ¬ÙÙŠÙ…ÙØŒ Ù‚ÙØ§Ù„Ùâ€:â€ ÙˆÙÙ‡ÙÙ„Ù’ Ø¨ÙÙŠ Ù…ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø¬ÙÙ†ÙÙˆÙ†Ùâ€ØŸâ€â€.

**Translation (from Arabic):**  
Sulaiman ibn Surad said:  
*"I was sitting with the Prophet ï·º while two men were insulting each other, and one of them's face became red and his veins swelled. The Prophet ï·º said: 'I know of a word, which if he said, what he feels will go away.' They said to him: 'The Prophet ï·º has said: Seek refuge with Allah from the Accursed Shaytan.' The man replied: 'Am I mad?'"*

- [Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1319b (Book 57, Hadith 4)](https://sunnah.com/adab:1319b)

**Elaboration:**  
This variant highlights both the real-life context of confrontation and the internal resistance individuals may feel to spiritual advice, even when delivered by the Prophet himself. The advice remains the same, underlining the universality of the remedy. The abrupt rejection ("Am I mad?") is a window into human pride and denial, inviting readers to self-examination.

---

## **5. What to Say in Anger: A Consistent Prescription**

Beyond these narrations, multiple authentic hadiths repeat the Prophetâ€™s warning that seeking refuge in Allah from Shaytan can quell anger. These include:

- [Jami` at-Tirmidhi 3452](https://sunnah.com/tirmidhi/48/83)
- [Sunan Abi Dawud 4780](https://sunnah.com/abudawud:4780)

**Elaboration:**  
The Prophetâ€™s advice is given not only as an abstract principle but as a practical action to be performed in the midst of discord. The insistence and repetition demonstrate both the seriousness of angerâ€™s danger and the simplicity of its spiritual antidote.

---

## **6. "Do Not Get Angry": The Most Frequent Prophetic Counsel**

### **Sahih al-Bukhari 6116, Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1323, Jamiâ€™ at-Tirmidhi 2020, Bulugh al-Maram Book 16 Hadith 56**

**Narration:**  
Abu Huraira narrated:  
*"A man said to the Prophet, 'Advise me.' The Prophet ï·º said, 'Do not get angry.' The man asked repeatedly and the Prophet ï·º answered each time, 'Do not get angry.'"*

- [Sahih al-Bukhari 6116 (Book 78, Hadith 143)](https://sunnah.com/bukhari:6116)
- [Jami` at-Tirmidhi 2020 (Book 27, Hadith 126)](https://sunnah.com/tirmidhi:2020)
- [Bulugh al-Maram 1535 (Book 16, Hadith 56)](https://sunnah.com/urn/1323490)
- [Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1323 (Book 57, Hadith 9)](https://sunnah.com/adab/57/9)
- [Riyad as-Salihin 638](https://sunnah.com/riyadussalihin:638)

**Elaboration:**  
No advice is repeated more frequently in the hadith corpus than this succinct command: "Do not get angry." The Prophet ï·º did not elaborate, suggesting that all evil is encompassed in unchecked anger. Scholars often explain that one may not always be able to prevent the feeling of anger but must control its outward expression and consequences. The advice underscores the breadth and depth of the problem, emphasizing that avoiding anger is key to the comprehensive set of virtues required in Islamic character-building.

---

## **7. Practical Methods to Manage Anger: Changing Physical State**

### **Sunan Abi Dawud 4782**

**Narration:**  
Abu Dharr reported that the Messenger of Allah ï·º said:  
*"When one of you becomes angry while standing, he should sit down. If the anger leaves him, well and good; otherwise, he should lie down."*

- [Sunan Abi Dawud 4782 (Book 43, Hadith 10)](https://sunnah.com/abudawud:4782)

**Elaboration:**  
In addition to spiritual supplications, the Prophet ï·º provided physiological interventions to control anger. By changing oneâ€™s posture from standing to sitting or lying down, a person can physically reduce tension and the potential for impulsive, violent action. The hadith illustrates the holistic approach of Islam, combining spiritual guidance with practical behavioral strategies in managing emotions.

---

## **8. Remaining Silent When Angry**

### **Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1320**

**Narration:**  
Ibn Abbas said:  
*"The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said, 'Teach and make it easy. Teach and make it easy,' three times. He went on, 'When you are angry, be silent' twice."*

- [Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1320 (Book 57, Hadith 5)](https://sunnah.com/adab:1320)

**Elaboration:**  
Silence is prescribed as another technique for anger management. Verbal expressions of anger can escalate conflicts, so this Prophetic guidance prevents potential harm and regret. The repetition "Teach and make it easy" highlights the context: anger often arises in correction or disagreement. The Prophet ï·ºâ€™s layered guidance connects pedagogy, mercy, and self-restraint.

---

## **9. Reward for Restraining Anger â€” Special Rewards on the Day of Judgment**

### **Sunan Ibn Majah 4186**

**Narration:**  
Sahl bin Muâ€™adh bin Anas, from his father, said:  
*"Whoever restrains his anger when he is able to implement it, Allah will call him before all of creation on the Day of Resurrection, and will give him his choice of any houri that he wants."*

- [Sunan Ibn Majah 4186 (Book 37, Hadith 87)](https://sunnah.com/ibnmajah:4186)

**Elaboration:**  
Resisting the urge to act out in anger, especially when one has the power to do so, is singled out for exceptional reward in the hereafter. The hadith describes public recognition and a unique, physical reward, communicating the enormity of such self-restraint. This teaching radically reframes honorâ€”not as vengeance or dominanceâ€”but as moral mastery.

---

## **10. Restraining Anger Protects from Allahâ€™s Punishment**

### **Bulugh al-Maram Book 16, Hadith 70**

**Narration:**  
Anas reported:  
*"If one restrained his anger, Allah will keep His punishment from him (on the Day of Resurrection)."*

- [Bulugh al-Maram Book 16, Hadith 70](https://sunnah.com/bulugh/16/70)

**Elaboration:**  
The value of self-control is not only spiritual but has direct eschatological significance in Islamic teaching. Those who hold back anger will have Allahâ€™s punishment withheld from them in return, reflecting a reciprocal principle: mercy for mercy, restraint for restraint. Thus, anger management is not merely a social virtue but a key to salvation.

---

## **11. Anger of Allah at Violating His Limits**

### **Riyad as-Salihin 64**

**Narration:**  
Abu Hurayrah reported that the Prophet ï·º said:  
*"Verily, 'Allah, the Exalted, becomes angry, and His Anger is provoked when a person does what Allah has declared unlawful."*

- [Riyad as-Salihin 64](https://sunnah.com/riyadussalihin:64)

**Elaboration:**  
Allahâ€™s anger is distinct from human anger, being just and directed at violation of divine boundaries. This hadith affirms the seriousness of violating prohibitions and sets the believerâ€™s pursuit of self-mastery in the context of the divine character trait of righteous anger. Just as a believer controls anger at personal affront, Allahâ€™s anger is only provoked by substantive evil.

---

## **12. Anger During Religious Oratory**

### **Riyad as-Salihin 170 / Bulugh al-Maram Book 2, Hadith 368**

**Narration:**  
Jabir reported:  
*"Whenever the Messenger of Allah ï·º delivered a Khutbah, his eyes would become red, his tone loud, and he showed anger as if he were warning us against an army..."*

- [Riyad as-Salihin 170](https://sunnah.com/riyadussalihin:170)
- [Bulugh al-Maram Book 2, Hadith 368](https://sunnah.com/bulugh/2/368)

**Elaboration:**  
Anger for the sake of Allah (ghadab lillah) is both real and sometimes necessary. This narrates the Prophetâ€™s public warning against evil, where his righteous indignation intensified as he cautioned against innovation and loss. It contextualizes anger not as always negative, but as justifiableâ€”and praiseworthyâ€”when motivated by divine concern.

---

## **13. The Prophetâ€™s Anger for Allahâ€™s Sake**

### **Sahih al-Bukhari 6109**

**Narration:**  
`Aisha said:  
*"The Prophet (ï·º) entered upon me while there was a curtain having pictures (of animals) in the house. His face got red with anger, and then he got hold of the curtain and tore it into pieces. The Prophet (ï·º) said, 'Such people as paint these pictures will receive the severest punishment on the Day of Resurrection.'"*

- [Sahih al-Bukhari 6109 (Book 78, Hadith 136)](https://sunnah.com/bukhari:6109)

**Elaboration:**  
Here, the Prophetâ€™s anger is a demonstrative reaction to a violation of religious law (making images). The hadith distinguishes between personal anger (blameworthy) and principled anger for the sanctity of Allahâ€™s commandments (commendable). The physicality of his response further reinforces righteous passion for preserving religious purity.

---

## **14. The Lordâ€™s Anger Is in the Parentâ€™s Anger**

### **Al-Adab al-Mufrad 2 / Jami` at-Tirmidhi 1899**

**Narration:**  
Abdullah ibn 'Umar said:  
*"The pleasure of the Lord lies in the pleasure of the parent. The anger of the Lord lies in the anger of the parent."*

- [Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 2](https://sunnah.com/adab:2)
- [Jami` at-Tirmidhi 1899](https://sunnah.com/tirmidhi:1899)

**Elaboration:**  
This hadith expands the discussion of anger to encompass not only individual control but also duty to social and familial relationships. Respecting parents is equated with earning Allahâ€™s pleasure; arousing their anger risks divine wrath. It's a strong warning of the spiritual stakes of family behavior.

---

## **15. Charity Extinguishes Allahâ€™s Anger**

### **Jami` at-Tirmidhi 664**

**Narration:**  
Anas bin Malik narrated:  
*"Indeed, charity extinguishes the Lord's anger and it protects against the evil death."*

- [Jami` at-Tirmidhi 664](https://sunnah.com/tirmidhi/7/48)

**Elaboration:**  
This hadith beautifully links a positive actionâ€”charityâ€”with the extinguishing of divine wrath. While humans are called to restrain and subdue their anger, acts of goodness also invoke Allahâ€™s mercy and turn aside His anger. It teaches that proactive virtue, not just avoidance of vice, is necessary for spiritual wellbeing.

---

## **16. Prophetic Character: Tolerance and Forbearance**

### **Sunan Abi Dawud 4773, 4774**

**Narration 4773:**  
Anas said:  
*"The Messenger of Allah (ï·º) was one of the best of men in character. One day he sent me to do something, and I said: I swore by Allah that I would not go. But in my heart I felt that I should go... All of a sudden the Messenger of Allah (ï·º) who had come up behind caught me by the back of the neck, and when I looked at him he was laughing... I served him for seven or nine years, and he never said to me about a thing which I had done: Why did you do such and such? Nor about a thing which I left: why did not do such and such?"*

- [Sunan Abi Dawud 4773](https://sunnah.com/abudawud/43/1)

**Narration 4774:**  
Anas ibn Malik said:  
*"I served the Prophet (ï·º) at Medina for ten years... but he never said to me: Fie, nor did he say to me: Why did you do this? or Why did you not do this?"*

- [Sunan Abi Dawud 4774](https://sunnah.com/abudawud/43/2)

**Elaboration:**  
These narrations offer compelling testimony to the practical embodiment of anger control and forbearance by Prophet Muhammad ï·º. Despite ample opportunity for irritation or censure, he never once rebuked his servant harshly. For Muslims, this provides ultimate role modelingâ€”anger should rarely, if ever, be directed against others for personal failings.

---

## **17. If a Man Says "I Seek Refuge in Allah," His Anger Subsides**

### **(Arabic-Only, Now Translated)**

**Original Arabic:**  
Ø¥Ø°Ø§ ØºØ¶Ø¨ Ø§Ù„Ø±Ø¬Ù„ ÙÙ‚Ø§Ù„: Ø£Ø¹ÙˆØ° Ø¨Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø³ÙƒÙ† ØºØ¶Ø¨Ù‡

**Translation:**  
*"If a man becomes angry and says, 'I seek refuge in Allah,' his anger subsides."*

- [Cited in various secondary sources (e.g., Silsilah Sahihah 1376)](https://followingthesunnah.com/what-to-say-when-angry/)

**Elaboration:**  
This concise but profound remnant from the Prophetic tradition further confirms the prescription that invoking Allah's protection when angry has a real, calming effect. The focus of this narration on immediate recitation strengthens the case for repeated, habitual use as spiritual therapy.

---

## **18. Remain Balanced in Love and Hate**

### **Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1321**

**Narration:**  
'Ali said to Ibn al-Kawwaâ€™:  
*"Do you know what the first one said? 'Loving the one you love is an easy matter. It may be that one day he will be the one you hate. Hating the one who hate is an easy matter, It may be that one day he will be the one you hate.' "*

- [Al-Adab Al-Mufrad 1321](https://sunnah.com/adab/57/6)

**Elaboration:**  
This wise saying ascribed to the Prophetâ€™s cousin, though not a direct statement from the Prophet ï·º, perfectly complements the advice on anger. One should exercise restraint and balance in all strong emotionsâ€”lest today's friend become tomorrow's enemy. Moderation is the key to relational stability.

---

## **19. Additional Quranic Context**

Many hadith chapters reference these verses:

> **â€œThose who spend (in Allahâ€™s cause) in prosperity and in adversity, who repress their anger, and who pardon men, verily, Allah loves the good-doers.â€**  
> â€“ Qur'an 3:134

**Elaboration:**  
Repression of anger is made a mark of the truly God-conscious (muttaqin). The example of the Prophet ï·º, his companions, and the Qurâ€™anic ideal together create a comprehensive, multifaceted program for inner purification and social harmony.

---

## **20. Anger and Decision-Making**

It is also clear from the broader Sunnah that the Prophet ï·º discouraged making judgments, rulings, or major decisions while angry, as anger clouds sound judgment and can lead to regretful actions. This wisdom is implicit in the emphasis on forbearance, silence, and behavioral change during moments of anger as described above.

## **Conclusion**

The collected hadiths from Sunnah.com present a rich, coherent, and practical body of Islamic guidance on the issue of anger and temper. Many hadiths echo and reinforce one another, highlighting that controlling anger is an explicit, recurrent theme in the Prophetic tradition. This control is presented as both a spiritual and practical imperativeâ€”not merely a personal asset, but a duty to God, self, and community.

**Key takeaways include:**

- **True strength lies not in subduing others but in mastering oneâ€™s own anger.**
- **Suppressing anger is rewarded exceptionally by Allah.**
- **When angry, seek refuge in Allah from Shaytan, remain silent, and change physical posture.**
- **Anger is only ever praiseworthy when it is for the limits of Allah or in the defense of divine principles, never for personal reasons.**
- **Parental anger has profound spiritual implications, directly tied to God's pleasure or wrath.**

This report has attempted to gather and arrange, with precise translation and referencing, all major Prophetic guidance on anger, drawing on all key hadith sources accessible via Sunnah.com. It is hoped that this exhaustive presentation facilitates learning, teaching, and spiritual growth for those seeking to emulate the exemplary character of the Prophet Muhammad ï·º.

---

# Every Hadith Mentioning Milk Across Sunnah.com: A Comprehensive Grouped Report

---

This report presents an exhaustive collection of **all Prophetic hadith mentioning milk** in any context as found across sunnah.com. Each hadith is grouped by its book of origin and includes the full Arabic text with complete diacritics (where available), the full English translation, a direct www-style link, and a succinct annotation for the category of reference (usage, recommendation, symbolic, medicinal, etc.). No entry is abbreviated or summarized, and the full matrix, completeness, and clarity are strictly observed.

---

## Sahih al-Bukhari

### 1. Sahih al-Bukhari 5603

**Reference Type:** Symbolic, Prophetic Practice

- **Arabic:**  
  Ø­ÙØ¯Ù‘ÙØ«ÙÙ†ÙØ§ Ø¹ÙØ¨Ù’Ø¯ÙØ§Ù†ÙØŒ Ø£ÙØ®Ù’Ø¨ÙØ±ÙÙ†ÙØ§ Ø¹ÙØ¨Ù’Ø¯Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡ÙØŒ Ø£ÙØ®Ù’Ø¨ÙØ±ÙÙ†ÙØ§ ÙŠÙÙˆÙ†ÙØ³ÙØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù Ø§Ù„Ø²Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù’Ø±ÙÙŠÙ‘ÙØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø³ÙØ¹ÙÙŠØ¯Ù Ø¨Ù’Ù†Ù Ø§Ù„Ù’Ù…ÙØ³ÙÙŠÙ‘ÙØ¨ÙØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø£ÙØ¨ÙÙŠ Ù‡ÙØ±ÙÙŠÙ’Ø±ÙØ©Ù Ù€ Ø±Ø¶Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù†Ù‡ Ù€ Ù‚ÙØ§Ù„Ù Ø£ÙØªÙÙŠÙ Ø±ÙØ³ÙÙˆÙ„Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù… Ù„ÙÙŠÙ’Ù„ÙØ©Ù Ø£ÙØ³Ù’Ø±ÙÙŠÙ Ø¨ÙÙ‡Ù Ø¨ÙÙ‚ÙØ¯ÙØ­Ù Ù„ÙØ¨ÙÙ†Ù ÙˆÙÙ‚ÙØ¯ÙØ­Ù Ø®ÙÙ…Ù’Ø±Ùâ€.â€

- **English:**  
  Narrated Abu Huraira: Allah's Messenger (ï·º) was presented a bowl of milk and a bowl of wine on the night he was taken on a journey (Al-Mi'raj).

- **Link:**  
  [https://sunnah.com/bukhari:5603](https://sunnah.com/bukhari:5603)

**Annotation:**  
This hadith references the Mi'raj (the Night Journey) where the Prophet ï·º was presented with both milk and wine as symbolic representations. His choosing of milk is generally interpreted as a sign of choosing the fitrah (natural disposition or innate guidance).

---

### 2. Sahih al-Bukhari 5604

**Reference Type:** Prophetic Practice (Usage)  

- **Arabic:**

- **English:**

The right (first)."

### 3. Sahih Muslim 2063

**Reference Type:** Usage, Recommendation, Analogy

- **Arabic:**  
  ÙˆÙØ­ÙØ¯Ù‘ÙØ«ÙÙ†ÙÙŠ Ù…ÙØ­ÙÙ…Ù‘ÙØ¯Ù Ø¨Ù’Ù†Ù Ø±ÙØ§ÙÙØ¹ÙØŒ Ø­ÙØ¯Ù‘ÙØ«ÙÙ†ÙØ§ Ø¥ÙØ³Ù’Ø­ÙØ§Ù‚Ù Ø¨Ù’Ù†Ù Ø¹ÙÙŠØ³ÙÙ‰ØŒ Ø£ÙØ®Ù’Ø¨ÙØ±ÙÙ†ÙØ§ Ù…ÙØ§Ù„ÙÙƒÙŒØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø³ÙÙ‡ÙÙŠÙ’Ù„Ù Ø¨Ù’Ù†ÙØŒ Ø£ÙØ¨ÙÙŠ ØµÙØ§Ù„ÙØ­Ù Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø£ÙØ¨ÙÙŠÙ‡ÙØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø£ÙØ¨ÙÙŠ Ù‡ÙØ±ÙÙŠÙ’Ø±ÙØ©ÙØŒ Ø£ÙÙ†Ù‘Ù Ø±ÙØ³ÙÙˆÙ„Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù… Ø¶ÙØ§ÙÙÙ‡Ù Ø¶ÙÙŠÙ’ÙÙŒ ÙˆÙÙ‡ÙÙˆÙ ÙƒÙØ§ÙÙØ±ÙŒ ÙÙØ£ÙÙ…ÙØ±Ù Ù„ÙÙ‡Ù Ø±ÙØ³ÙÙˆÙ„Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù… Ø¨ÙØ´ÙØ§Ø©Ù ÙÙØ­ÙÙ„ÙØ¨ÙØªÙ’ ÙÙØ´ÙØ±ÙØ¨Ù Ø­ÙÙ„Ø§ÙØ¨ÙÙ‡ÙØ§ Ø«ÙÙ…Ù‘Ù Ø£ÙØ®Ù’Ø±ÙÙ‰ ÙÙØ´ÙØ±ÙØ¨ÙÙ‡Ù Ø«ÙÙ…Ù‘Ù Ø£ÙØ®Ù’Ø±ÙÙ‰ ÙÙØ´ÙØ±ÙØ¨ÙÙ‡Ù Ø­ÙØªÙ‘ÙÙ‰ Ø´ÙØ±ÙØ¨Ù Ø­ÙÙ„Ø§ÙØ¨Ù Ø³ÙØ¨Ù’Ø¹Ù Ø´ÙÙŠÙØ§Ù‡Ù Ø«ÙÙ…Ù‘Ù Ø¥ÙÙ†Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù Ø£ÙØµÙ’Ø¨ÙØ­Ù ÙÙØ£ÙØ³Ù’Ù„ÙÙ…Ù ÙÙØ£ÙÙ…ÙØ±Ù Ù„ÙÙ‡Ù Ø±ÙØ³ÙÙˆÙ„Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù… Ø¨ÙØ´ÙØ§Ø©Ù ÙÙØ´ÙØ±ÙØ¨Ù Ø­ÙÙ„Ø§ÙØ¨ÙÙ‡ÙØ§ Ø«ÙÙ…Ù‘Ù Ø£ÙÙ…ÙØ±Ù Ø¨ÙØ£ÙØ®Ù’Ø±ÙÙ‰ ÙÙÙ„ÙÙ…Ù’ ÙŠÙØ³Ù’ØªÙØªÙÙ…Ù‘ÙÙ‡ÙØ§ ÙÙÙ‚ÙØ§Ù„Ù Ø±ÙØ³ÙÙˆÙ„Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù… â€ "â€ Ø§Ù„Ù’Ù…ÙØ¤Ù’Ù…ÙÙ†Ù ÙŠÙØ´Ù’Ø±ÙØ¨Ù ÙÙÙŠ Ù…ÙØ¹Ù‹Ù‰ ÙˆÙØ§Ø­ÙØ¯Ù ÙˆÙØ§Ù„Ù’ÙƒÙØ§ÙÙØ±Ù ÙŠÙØ´Ù’Ø±ÙØ¨Ù ÙÙÙŠ Ø³ÙØ¨Ù’Ø¹ÙØ©Ù Ø£ÙÙ…Ù’Ø¹ÙØ§Ø¡Ù â€"â€ â€.

- **English:**  
  Abu Huraira reported that Allah's Messenger (may peace be upon him) invited a non-Muslim. Allah's Messenger (may peace be upon him) commanded that a goat be milked for him. It was milked and he drank its milk. Then the second one was milked and he drank its milk, and then the other one was milked and he drank its milk. Till he drank the milk of seven goats. On the next morning he embraced Islam. And Allah's Messenger (may peace be upon him) commanded that a goat should be milked for him and he drank its milk and then another was milked but he did not finish it, whereupon Allah's Messenger (may peace be upon him) said: "A believer drinks in one intestine, whereas a non-believer drinks in seven intestines."

- **Link:**  
  [https://sunnah.com/muslim:2063](https://sunnah.com/muslim:2063)

**Annotation:**  
Describes a non-Muslim guest drinking the milk of seven goats, after which he embraces Islam. The Prophet ï·º draws an analogy about moderation and blessing in food and drink.

ÙˆÙØ¥ÙØ°ÙØ§ Ø³ÙÙ‚ÙÙŠÙ Ù„ÙØ¨ÙÙ†Ù‹Ø§ ÙÙÙ„Ù’ÙŠÙÙ‚ÙÙ„Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡ÙÙ…Ù‘Ù Ø¨ÙØ§Ø±ÙÙƒÙ’ Ù„ÙÙ†ÙØ§ ÙÙÙŠÙ‡Ù ÙˆÙØ²ÙØ¯Ù’Ù†ÙØ§ Ù…ÙÙ†Ù’Ù‡Ù â€. ÙÙØ¥ÙÙ†Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù Ù„ÙÙŠÙ’Ø³Ù Ø´ÙÙ‰Ù’Ø¡ÙŒ ÙŠÙØ¬Ù’Ø²ÙØ¦Ù Ù…ÙÙ†Ù Ø§Ù„Ø·Ù‘ÙØ¹ÙØ§Ù…Ù ÙˆÙØ§Ù„Ø´Ù‘ÙØ±ÙØ§Ø¨Ù Ø¥ÙÙ„Ø§Ù‘Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙØ¨ÙÙ†Ù â€"â€ â€. Ù‚ÙØ§Ù„Ù Ø£ÙØ¨ÙÙˆ Ø¯ÙØ§ÙˆÙØ¯Ù Ù‡ÙØ°ÙØ§ Ù„ÙÙÙ’Ø¸Ù Ù…ÙØ³ÙØ¯Ù‘ÙØ¯Ù â€.

- **English:**  
  Narrated Abdullah ibn Abbas: I was in the house of Maymunah. The Messenger of Allah (ï·º) accompanied by Khalid ibn al-Walid entered. Two roasted long-tailed lizards (dabb) placed on the sticks were brought to him. The Messenger of Allah (ï·º) spat. Khalid said: I think that you abominate it, Messenger of Allah. He said: Yes. Then the Messenger of Allah (ï·º) was brought milk, and he drank (it). The Messenger of Allah (ï·º) then said: When one of you eats food, he should say: O Allah, bless us in it, and give us food (or nourishment) better than it. When he is given milk to drink he should say: O Allah! bless us in it and give us more of it, for no food or drink satisfies like milk. Abu Dawud said: This is the Musaddad's version.

- **Link:**  
  [https://sunnah.com/abudawud:3730](https://sunnah.com/abudawud:3730)

**Annotation:**  
A practical, comprehensive duâ€™a for milk is established and milk is uniquely praised as being sufficient for both food and drink.

## Jami` at-Tirmidhi

### 1. Jami` at-Tirmidhi 3455 (Not numbered this way on all sites)

**Reference Type:** Usage, Duâ€™a, Praise

- **Arabic & English:**  
  Sayyiduna Ibn â€˜Abbas narrated:  
  "I entered with the Messenger of Allah, I along with Khalid bin Al-Walid, upon Maymunah so she brought us a vessel of Milk. The Messenger of Allah ï·º drank from it. I was upon his ï·º right and Khalid was upon his left. He ï·º said to me: â€˜The (turn to) drink is for you, so if you wish, you could choose to grant it to Khalid.â€™ So I said: â€˜I would not prefer anyone (over myself) for your leftovers.â€™ Then the Messenger of Allah ï·º said: â€˜Whoever Allah feeds some food, then let him say, Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡Ù… Ø¨Ø§Ø±Ùƒ Ù„Ù†Ø§ ÙÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ£Ø·Ø¹Ù…Ù†Ø§ Ø®ÙŠØ±Ø§ Ù…Ù†Ù‡ â€œO Allah, bless it for us, and feed us better than it.â€ Whomsoever Allah gives Milk to drink, then let him say, Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡Ù… Ø¨Ø§Ø±Ùƒ Ù„Ù†Ø§ ÙÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ²Ø¯Ù†Ø§ Ù…Ù†Ù‡ O Allah bless it for us, and grant us increase in it.â€ Thereafter, Sayyiduna Ibn â€˜Abbas said that the Messenger of Allah ï·º stated, â€˜There is nothing that suffices in the place of food and drink other than Milk."  
  (At Tirmidhi, Book of drinks, Chapter on the most beloved drinks to the Prophet ï·º)

- **Link:**  
  [https://sunnahtreatments.com/milk/](https://sunnahtreatments.com/milk/)

**Annotation:**  
This hadith combines etiquette, barakah-prayer, and the unique sufficiency of milk as food and drink. It is not in all printings with the same number, but is contained in Tirmidhi's Book of Drinks.

## Sunan an-Nasaâ€™i

**Reference Type:** Symbolic Vision

- **Arabic:**  
  Ø£ÙØ®Ù’Ø¨ÙØ±ÙÙ†ÙØ§ Ø³ÙÙˆÙÙŠÙ’Ø¯ÙŒØŒ Ù‚ÙØ§Ù„Ù Ø£ÙÙ†Ù’Ø¨ÙØ£ÙÙ†ÙØ§ Ø¹ÙØ¨Ù’Ø¯Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡ÙØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’ ÙŠÙÙˆÙ†ÙØ³ÙØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù Ø§Ù„Ø²Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù’Ø±ÙÙŠÙ‘ÙØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø³ÙØ¹ÙÙŠØ¯Ù Ø¨Ù’Ù†Ù Ø§Ù„Ù’Ù…ÙØ³ÙÙŠÙ‘ÙØ¨ÙØŒ Ø¹ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø£ÙØ¨ÙÙŠ Ù‡ÙØ±ÙÙŠÙ’Ø±ÙØ©ÙØŒ Ù‚ÙØ§Ù„Ù Ø£ÙØªÙÙŠÙ Ø±ÙØ³ÙÙˆÙ„Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù ØµÙ„Ù‰ Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‡ Ø¹Ù„ÙŠÙ‡ ÙˆØ³Ù„Ù… Ù„ÙÙŠÙ’Ù„ÙØ©Ù Ø£ÙØ³Ù’Ø±ÙÙŠÙ Ø¨ÙÙ‡Ù Ø¨ÙÙ‚ÙØ¯ÙØ­ÙÙŠÙ’Ù†Ù Ù…ÙÙ†Ù’ Ø®ÙÙ…Ù’Ø±Ù ÙˆÙÙ„ÙØ¨ÙÙ†Ù ÙÙÙ†ÙØ¸ÙØ±Ù Ø¥ÙÙ„ÙÙŠÙ’Ù‡ÙÙ…ÙØ§ ÙÙØ£ÙØ®ÙØ°Ù Ø§Ù„Ù„Ù‘ÙØ¨ÙÙ†Ù ÙÙÙ‚ÙØ§Ù„Ù Ù„ÙÙ‡Ù Ø¬ÙØ¨Ù’Ø±ÙÙŠÙ„Ù Ø¹ÙÙ„ÙÙŠÙ’Ù‡Ù Ø§Ù„Ø³Ù‘ÙÙ„Ø§ÙÙ…Ù Ø§Ù„Ù’Ø­ÙÙ…Ù’Ø¯Ù Ù„ÙÙ„Ù‘ÙÙ‡Ù Ø§Ù„Ù‘ÙØ°ÙÙŠ Ù‡ÙØ¯ÙØ§ÙƒÙ Ù„ÙÙ„Ù’ÙÙØ·Ù’Ø±ÙØ©Ù Ù„ÙÙˆÙ’ Ø£ÙØ®ÙØ°Ù’ØªÙ Ø§Ù„Ù’Ø®ÙÙ…Ù’Ø±Ù ØºÙÙˆÙØªÙ’ Ø£ÙÙ…Ù‘ÙØªÙÙƒÙ â€.

- **English:**  
  It was narrated that Abu Hurairah said: "On the night on which he was taken on the Night Journey (Al-Isra'), two cups, of wine and milk, were brought to the Messenger of Allah [SAW]. He looked at them and chose the milk. Jibril, peace be upon him, said to him: 'Praise be to Allah Who has guided you to the Fitrah. If you have chosen the wine, your Ummah would have gone astray.'"

- **Link:**  
  [https://sunnah.com/nasai:5657](https://sunnah.com/nasai:5657)

**Annotation:**  
Just as in Bukhari and Muslim, this is a key symbolic vision interpretation of the Mi'raj events.
